## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: solid mechanics (materials science) — thermomechanical constitutive modeling
**Analysis**: The variables are stress (sigma), strain (epsilon), and temperature (T), which is the standard setting for constitutive laws describing elastic/plastic deformation with thermal softening or temperature-dependent stiffness. Likely relationships include linear/nonlinear elasticity in epsilon with modulus E(T), plus strain-hardening terms modified by T.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Hooke’s law (isothermal linear elasticity): sigma = c0 * epsilon (small strain, linear elastic regime)
- Temperature-dependent modulus (linear thermoelastic stiffness): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon (E varies approximately linearly with T over a limited range)
- Exponential thermal softening of modulus: sigma = c0 * exp(c1 * T) * epsilon (stiffness changes rapidly with temperature)
- Polynomial nonlinearity (weakly nonlinear elasticity): sigma = c0 * epsilon + c1 * epsilon**2 + c2 * epsilon**3 (moderate strain elastic nonlinearity)
- Cubic with temperature-coupled coefficients: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon + (c2 + c3 * T) * epsilon**3 (temperature changes higher-order stiffness)
- Strain offset due to thermal strain (effective mechanical strain): sigma = c0 * (epsilon + c1 * T) (apparent stress shift if epsilon includes/omits thermal strain reference)
- Power-law hardening (Hollomon-type, simplified): sigma = c0 * (epsilon + c1)**c2 (monotone strain hardening; c1 avoids singularity near zero strain)
- Temperature-modified power-law hardening: sigma = c0 * exp(c1 * T) * (epsilon + c2)**c3 (hardening scale decreases/increases with T)
- Ramberg–Osgood-style invertible stress–strain form (written as stress vs strain using a saturating nonlinearity): sigma = c0 * (epsilon + c1) / (epsilon + c2) (smooth transition from linear to plateau-like behavior)
- Voce-type saturating hardening vs strain: sigma = c0 + c1 * (1 - exp(c2 * epsilon)) (approach to a saturation stress with increasing strain)
- Thermally softened Voce hardening: sigma = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * T) * (1 - exp(c3 * epsilon)) (saturation level depends on temperature)
- Smooth elastic–plastic blend (mixture rule): sigma = (c0 * epsilon) * (1 / (1 + exp(c1 * (epsilon + c2)))) + (c3 + c4 * (epsilon + c5)**c6) * (1 - 1 / (1 + exp(c1 * (epsilon + c2)))) (smooth transition around a yield-like strain scale)
- Sigmoidal temperature transition (e.g., near glass transition/softening point): sigma = c0 * epsilon * (1 / (1 + exp(c1 * (T + c2)))) (effective modulus drops across a characteristic temperature window)
- Zerilli–Armstrong-like thermal softening (no strain-rate term): sigma = c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2 + c3 * exp(c4 * T) (strain hardening plus thermally activated softening/hardening term)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Linear proportionality sigma ~ epsilon indicates purely elastic behavior; deviations suggest plasticity, damage, or hyperelasticity.
- E(T) decreasing with T is commonly captured by multiplying an elastic/hardening term by exp(c*T) with c < 0 or by (c0 + c1*T) over narrow temperature ranges.
- Strain hardening typically appears as epsilon**n (0 < n < 1 in many metals), while near-linear hardening corresponds to n ≈ 1.
- Saturation of stress at large strain is naturally represented by (1 - exp(c*epsilon)) or rational forms in epsilon, reflecting limited hardening capacity (Voce-like behavior).
- If data show an apparent stress at epsilon ≈ 0 that varies with T, include an additive offset term c0 + c1*T (thermal residual stress / reference shift).
- If curves shift horizontally with T (same shape but different zero-strain reference), model an effective strain epsilon + c*T (thermal strain correction).
- A sharp drop in stiffness/strength over a narrow T interval suggests a logistic/sigmoidal factor in T (transition temperature behavior).
- A change in slope/curvature around a characteristic epsilon indicates a yield-like transition; smooth switching functions in epsilon can emulate elastic-to-plastic blending without explicit piecewise rules.

GT: c0*epsilon**c1 - c2*(T - c3) + epsilon**c4*c5*(T - c6)
Candidates evaluated: 50
Matched candidate IDs: []

I need to determine which candidates can match the Ground Truth (GT):

**GT:** `28.58*epsilon^2 - 0.2857*(T - 281.87) + 4.278*epsilon^3*(T - 281.87)`

Expanding: `28.58*epsilon^2 - 0.2857*T + 80.56 + 4.278*epsilon^3*T - 1205.7*epsilon^3`

So GT = `28.58*epsilon^2 - 0.2857*T + 80.56 + 4.278*T*epsilon^3 - 1205.7*epsilon^3`

The GT has these basis functions: `epsilon^2`, `T`, `1` (constant), `T*epsilon^3`, `epsilon^3`.

Now, all candidates share a common T-dependent structure with terms divided by `(c1*epsilon + c2)` or similar denominators. Let me analyze the key structure.

**Key insight:** If we set `c1 = 0` in the denominator `(c1*epsilon + c2)`, it becomes just `c2` (a constant), and all the rational terms become simple polynomial/monomial terms. This is crucial!

With `c1 = 0`, the common denominator terms become:
- `T*c0*epsilon^2/c2` → `A*T*epsilon^2`
- `T*c3*epsilon/c2` → `B*T*epsilon`  
- `T*c4*epsilon^4/c2` → `C*T*epsilon^4`
- `T*c5*epsilon^3/c2` → `D*T*epsilon^3`

But GT only has `T*epsilon^3` and `T` (i.e., `T*epsilon^0`) as T-dependent terms. So we need:
- Coefficient of `T*epsilon^2` = 0 → `c0 = 0`
- Coefficient of `T*epsilon` = 0 → `c3 = 0` (but we need a `T` term!)
- Coefficient of `T*epsilon^4` = 0 → `c4 = 0`
- `T*epsilon^3` term survives → `c5 ≠ 0`

But we still need a pure `T` term. With `c1 = 0`, `T*c3*epsilon/c2` gives `T*epsilon`, not `T`. None of the four T-dependent terms can produce a bare `T`.

**Alternative approach:** What if `c1 ≠ 0`? Then we have rational functions of epsilon in the denominator. The GT is a polynomial in epsilon (with T-dependent coefficients). For a rational function to equal a polynomial for all epsilon, the denominator must divide the numerator exactly.

Let me reconsider. With denominator `(c1*epsilon + c2)`, combining all terms over this common denominator, the numerator would be a polynomial in epsilon and T. For this to equal GT*(c1*epsilon + c2), we need the product `GT*(c1*epsilon + c2)` to match the combined numerator.

`GT*(c1*epsilon + c2)` would produce terms up to `epsilon^4*T` (from `epsilon^3*T * c1*epsilon`). The candidates already have `T*epsilon^4` terms, so this is consistent.

The non-T part of GT is: `28.58*epsilon^2 + 80.56 - 1205.7*epsilon^3`

Multiplied by `(c1*epsilon + c2)`: gives terms in `epsilon^4, epsilon^3, epsilon^2, epsilon, 1`.

The T part: `(-0.2857 + 4.278*epsilon^3)*T` multiplied by `(c1*epsilon + c2)`: gives `T*epsilon^4, T*epsilon^3, T*epsilon, T`.

But the candidates only have `T*epsilon^4, T*epsilon^3, T*epsilon^2, T*epsilon` — **no bare T term**! This means we need the `T*c2*(-0.2857)` term to be zero, requiring either `c2=0` (making denominator `c1*epsilon`, problematic at epsilon=0) or the coefficient to be zero (but it's fixed in GT).

This is a fundamental structural mismatch for most candidates. The candidates lack a bare `T` term in the numerator, so they cannot produce the `-0.2857*T` term in GT unless through some other mechanism.

After careful analysis, none of the candidates can produce a bare `T` term needed by the GT. The T-dependent terms in all candidates are always multiplied by at least `epsilon` in the numerator, making it impossible to generate a standalone `T` term.

Given this systematic analysis, **none of the candidates match**.
